    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="da7412b76a354212baf15fbcfa530d1e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '0794ed78-3809-4088-8bf0-decbed64c62f'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>Shanghai Institute of Ceramics, Chinese Academy of Sciences, Shanghai, 200050 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Yang/Cheng">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Liu/Yu" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a6" data-db-target-for="a6" aria-controls="a6" aria-haspopup="true" id="a6_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Prof. Yu Liu<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a6" aria-labelledby="a6_Ctrl" role="region" id="a6">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Prof. Yu Liu</p>

                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:yuliu@mail.sic.ac.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>yuliu@mail.sic.ac.cn</span></a></li>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d134249855" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies regulation is proposed to achieve high specific capacity of Prussian blue analogues (PBAs). And then a novel precipitation conversion synthesis method was designed. The as-prepared PBAs with hierarchical hollow morphology and [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies have reached a unprecedent level of high capacity, superior rate performance, and ultrahigh energy density.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7cfa6e9e-0212-4aed-bee4-0d2a3595d3f9/anie202400214-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7cfa6e9e-0212-4aed-bee4-0d2a3595d3f9/anie202400214-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4b0e0741-2054-4003-bc10-ef14495e2f4a/anie202400214-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7cfa6e9e-0212-4aed-bee4-0d2a3595d3f9/anie202400214-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d134249890" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Prussian blue analogues (PBAs) have been widely applied in many fields, especially as cathode materials of sodium-ion batteries on account of their low cost and open framework for fast ions transport. However, the capacity of reported PBAs has a great distance from its theoretical value. Herein, we proposed that [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies are crucial point for the high specific capacity for the first time. The [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies may create net electrons and reduce obstacles to ionic transport, which is conducive to rate performance of PBAs by increasing electronic and ionic conductivity to some extent. As a proof of concept, a series of PBAs have been prepared by co-precipitation method. And then, a novel precipitation conversion method has been designed, by which unique PBAs with a specific quantity of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies was successfully synthesized. Remarkably, the as-prepared PBAs possessing hierarchical hollow morphology have reached a unprecedent level of high capacity (168 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 25 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, close to PBAs’ theoretical capacity 170 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), high rate performance (90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and high energy density (over 500 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202400214-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202400214-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>After decades of development, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely used in consumer electronics, new energy vehicles, and other fields. However, LIBs cannot meet the growing demand in the field of large-scale energy storage, limited by the uneven distribution of lithium resources and low resource content on the earth.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0001_R_d134249765e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a></sup></span> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) not only have the advantages of high resource content and low materials cost, but also possess similar electrochemical properties to LIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0002_R_d134249765e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a></sup></span> Therefore, SIBs have obtained a wide range of attention by the academic and industry research in recent years.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0003_R_d134249765e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">3</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>There are several main kinds of cathode materials of SIBs, layered metal oxides, Prussian blue analogues (PBAs), polyanions, and organic compounds. PBAs have high theoretical capacity, low cost, and open framework, which are considered as the promising materials of SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0004_R_d134249765e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Small">4</a></sup></span> The chemical formula of PBAs is A<sub><i>x</i></sub>B[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub><i>y</i></sub> ⋅ □<sub><i>1-y</i></sub> ⋅ <i>z</i>H<sub>2</sub>O (A: alkali ions, 0&lt;<i>x</i>&lt;2, B: transition metal ions, 0&lt;<i>y</i>&lt;1, □: [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancy). PBAs contain some lattice defects (mainly [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancy) inevitably. The conventional viewpoints hold that the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies have a negative impact on the capacity and electronic conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0005_R_d134249765e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Storage Mater.">5</a>-<a href="#anie202400214-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0007_R_d134249765e470" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">7</a></sup></span> Because the vacancies may reduce the amount of active redox pairs (Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>) and bring higher Ohmic polarization.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0008_R_d134249765e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">8</a></sup></span> However, some inadequacies can be found, when digging deeper into the two viewpoints. First, the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies reduce the amount of active redox pairs and unit mass at the same time. And the Fe element connecting with only single [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] can also change its valence state, which are quiet common in non-stoichiometric semiconductor.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0009_R_d134249765e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nanotechnology">9</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0010_R_d134249765e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Symp. Ser.">10</a></sup></span> Therefore, the theoretical specific capacity will increase with the increase of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies actually, after simple calculations. Second, the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies make the whole crystal short of negative charge, which can be balanced by cation vacancies and net electron supplement. In this way, the electron concentration may increase a lot, which contribute to improve the electronic conductivity. Furthermore, the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies facilitate the ionic transport by reducing obstacles to ionic transport.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0011_R_d134249765e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span> Based on the above analysis, the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies may increase the theoretical specific capacity, electronic and ionic conductivity.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we proposed that [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies play an important role on the improvement of capacity and rate performance of PBAs cathode for the first time. As a proof of concept, a series of PBAs have been prepared by co-precipitation method. And then, a novel precipitation conversion method have been designed, by which unique PBAs with a specific quantity of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies was successfully synthesized. Remarkably, the as-prepared PBAs possessing hierarchical hollow morphology have reached a unprecedent level of high capacity (168 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 25 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, close to PBAs’ theoretical capacity 170 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), high rate performance (90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and high energy density (over 500 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>). The unconventional vacancies strategy and synthesis method could inspire different field of researches relating to PBAs or other materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202400214-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202400214-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Theoretical capacity of PBAs is increasing with the increase of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0001">1a</a>), based on chemical formula of Na<sub>4<i>x</i>-2</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub><i>x</i></sub> (0&lt;<i>x</i>&lt;1). Moreover, if electrons occupied [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies to make the whole PBAs electrically neutral, electronic conductivity of PBAs could be improved by the high electron concentration (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0001">1b</a>). And the increase of vacancies may decrease obstruction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions transport, which may enhance the rate performance to some extent. As a proof of concept, a series of PBAs have been prepared with different [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies but similar morphology. The different [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies were regulated by different Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4−</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> molar ratio, as shown in Table S1. According to thermogravimetric (TG) and inductively coupled plasma mass spectrometry (ICP) analysis (Figure S1 and Table S2), the PBAs contain more [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies as the lower molar ratio of Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4−</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup>, which is confirmed by X-ray diffraction (XRD) Rietveld refinement (Figure S2, S3 and Table S3~S5). There is no significant difference of these three samples (Figure S4), all of which exhibit irregular shapes. The electrochemical properties are displayed in Figure S5. Obviously, the more [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies PBAs sample possesses, the higher specific capacity it shows at small current density. And the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies have an optimal influence on the rate performance. Small content of the vacancies may be conducive to the rate performance, but instead of high content of the vacancies.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202400214-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e0b3d3a3-8bae-43f4-99cf-d53f96a29291/anie202400214-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e0b3d3a3-8bae-43f4-99cf-d53f96a29291/anie202400214-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ccbef437-99eb-4f75-8223-d4b08aaab12e/anie202400214-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e0b3d3a3-8bae-43f4-99cf-d53f96a29291/anie202400214-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400214-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400214" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Preparation of PBAs. (a) Relationship between theoretical specific capacity of Na<sub>4<i>x</i>-2</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub><i>x</i></sub> and proportion of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]; (b) Schematic diagram of fast ion and electron transportation caused by Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> vacancies; (c) Schematic diagram of the preparation principle of 0.004 mol Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and FeCl<sub>2</sub> ⋅ 4H<sub>2</sub>O in 45 mL Eg Solvent.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In traditional co-precipitation preparation method of PBAs, it is necessary to add complexing agents or reduce the salt concentration directly to reduce the local concentration of the precipitated reactants, for controlling the rate of grain growth. These will lead to abuse of industrial wastewater and complexing agent. The solvable salts concentration can also be controlled by selecting the type of solvent, where the salts are difficult to dissolve in the solvent (generally organic solvents). There exists a dissolution-precipitation balance of the difficult-dissolved slats in the solvent, such as Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> in ethylene glycol (Eg) solvent (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0001">1c</a>). Therefore, it naturally creates a specific low reaction concentration. The preparation of PBAs is essentially a precipitation conversion reaction. Two difficult-dissolved substances tend to produce more difficult-dissolved substance in the solvent. The reaction extent and rate depend on the type of reactants and solvent. Such an experimental design not only controls the concentration of reactants, but also avoids the abuse of complexing agents and wastewater, which is very promising for industrial applications.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">FeCl<sub>2</sub> ⋅ 4H<sub>2</sub>O, FeSO<sub>4</sub> ⋅ 7H<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, and Eg were selected for the preparation of PBAs. The solubility of these salts and reaction products is shown in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#anie202400214-tbl-0001">1</a> in Eg solvent. The PBAs (NaFeHCF) is less soluble than Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, and the sodium salts (NaCl, Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>) are both less soluble than the corresponding iron salts (FeCl<sub>2</sub> ⋅ 4H<sub>2</sub>O, FeSO<sub>4</sub> ⋅ 7H<sub>2</sub>O). Therefore, PBAs can be synthesized by chemical reaction Equation 1 or 2. The specific procedure of the synthesis process is shown in Figure S6. A certain amount of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and metal salts (FeCl<sub>2</sub> ⋅ 4H<sub>2</sub>O, FeSO<sub>4</sub> ⋅ 7H<sub>2</sub>O) were directly mixed in the Eg solvent and stirred in water bath. Finally, the PBAs powder was obtained by centrifugal washing and vacuum drying. If the reactants (Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, FeCl<sub>2</sub>, and FeSO<sub>4</sub>) do not contain crystalline water, it can avoid the involvement of water in the reaction process. And Eg can dissolve enough FeCl<sub>2</sub>, which could be used as a solvent to wash the prepared PBAs samples. In this way, the precipitation conversion method may realize anhydrous PBAs in the preparation process, which is a future exploration to avoid the battery inflation or bulging caused by water decomposition.

         <p>In this research, the amount of solvents and salts were 45 mL, 0.004 mol (1.216 g Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, 0.795 g FeCl<sub>2</sub> ⋅ 4H<sub>2</sub>O and 1.112 g FeSO<sub>4</sub> ⋅ 7H<sub>2</sub>O). Based on these maximum dissolved masses (Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#anie202400214-tbl-0001">1</a>), the reactions in this study can be written strictly as chemical reaction equations in Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0001">1c</a> (marked as Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub>) and Equation S1 (marked as Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub>), respectively. The Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4−</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> ratio in the FeCl<sub>2</sub> ⋅ 4H<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> saturated Eg solution are much lower than that in the FeSO<sub>4</sub> ⋅ 7H<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> saturated Eg solution, from the different solubility in Eg solvent (Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#anie202400214-tbl-0001">1</a>). And the solubility affects dissolution rate, which realize the mechanism of regulating the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies finally, as above experiment. If the structure of PBAs is considered to be perfect and is completely dissociated as: Na<sup>+</sup>, Fe<sup>2+</sup>, [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4−</sup> in Eg solvent. Then the equilibrium constant (<i>K<sub>c</sub></i>) for each chemical reaction can be calculated as 2.4×10<sup>5</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0001">1c</a>), 3.7×10<sup>10</sup> (Equation S1) respectively. The tendency or extent of the both reactions are large, judging from the <i>K<sub>c</sub></i> of each reaction. In addition, the low solubility of slats controls the concentration of reactants and reaction rate. Differences in by-products and reaction equilibrium constants may result in the final difference on the morphology and electrochemical properties of the synthesized PBAs.</p>
         
         <p>According to the XRD Rietveld refinement results (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0002">2a</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0002">2b</a>), the both samples display a face-centered cubic structure (Fm-3 m). And the lattice parameters are relatively close to each other, 10.234 Å, and 10.231 Å, respectively (Table S6, S7). The [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> and Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> samples are confirmed according to the XRD Rietveld refinement results (Figure S7a and S7b). The [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies are occupied by interstitial water, corresponding to the O element occupying the position of N element in the refined structure. And the interstitial water is also located on the Na vacancies, similar to previous reports.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0008_R_d134249765e1152" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">8</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0012_R_d134249765e1152" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a></sup></span> The number of Na and [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies of the XRD Rietveld refinement results is close to the results of ICP analysis (Table S6 ~ S8). According to the results of TG analysis (Figure S8), the water content of the two samples is 17.1 % and 16.1 % until 250 °C, respectively. Based on the results of ICP and TG, the samples can be denoted as Na<sub>0.64</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.52</sub> ⋅ □<sub>0.48</sub> ⋅ 2.08H<sub>2</sub>O (Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub>), Na<sub>0.69</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.70</sub> ⋅ □<sub>0.30</sub> ⋅ 2.34H<sub>2</sub>O (Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub>). The theoretical specific capacities of these two samples are 186 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub>) and 173 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub>) respectively, assuming that the valence state of Fe element could change completely.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202400214-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6695f47f-64b0-460b-9e76-bf5675f8b6a5/anie202400214-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6695f47f-64b0-460b-9e76-bf5675f8b6a5/anie202400214-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c6b605ad-b101-4a64-9e8b-a0eddd022f9e/anie202400214-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6695f47f-64b0-460b-9e76-bf5675f8b6a5/anie202400214-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400214-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400214" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Spectroscopy and morphological characterization. (a), (b) XRD pattern of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> and Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> respectively; (c), (d) MIBE-XPS analysis of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> and Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub>; (e) EPR spectra of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> and Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub>; (f), (g) SEM images of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub>; (h) TEM images Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub>; (i) SEM image of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> and corresponding element (Na, Fe, C, N, O) mapping.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Monatomic ion beam etching X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (MIBE-XPS) can not only gain insights into the chemical states and local structure of Fe at a bulk level, but also study different element variations at different depth of PBAs. Therefore, MIBE-XPS were employed to investigate Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> and Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> PBAs samples at different depths (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0002">2c</a> and <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0002">2d</a>). Split-peak fitting of the MIBE-XPS measurements data yield peak positions of Fe(+2) at 708.6 eV, 721.5 eV. The peak positions of Fe(+3) were at 710.4 eV, 723.8 eV, which were approximate to those reported literature.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0013_R_d134249765e1247" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - CORE AND VALENCE LEVEL PHOTOEMISSION STUDIES OF IRON OXIDE SURFACES AND THE OXIDATION OF IRON">13</a>-<a href="#anie202400214-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0016_R_d134249765e1247" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Mater. Chem. A">16</a></sup></span> In addition to the main peak, both Fe2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Fe2p<sub>3/2</sub> contain numerous satellite peaks. There appears new characteristic peak of Fe (0) 2P after etch, which results from Ar<sup>+</sup> etch in the MIBE-XPS.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0017_R_d134249765e1257" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Mater. Chem. Front.">17</a></sup></span> The ratio of Fe(+2) and Fe(+3) of the two samples decreases obviously after etch, which demonstrates that there is more Fe(+2) in the outer layer and more Fe(+3) in the inner layer. And the ratio of Fe(+2) and Fe(+3) does not appear much difference, as the depth of inner layer increases. The Na content on the surface is higher than that on the inner layer, in MIBE-XPS full spectra of the two samples (Figure S9a~S6d). This illustrates that the Na content is enriched on the surface of the two samples, resulting in a lower valence state of Fe in the outer layer. It is worth noting that the O content is gathered on the surface of the two samples, which only appears a small difference in the inner layer (Figure S9c and S9d). This should be attributed to more adsorbed water on the surface of the PBAs samples.</p>
         
         <p>Electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) spectra detects situation of unpaired electrons, which is used to study coordination environment and valence state of transition metals. According to the EPR spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0002">2e</a> and Figure S10), the characteristic peak with around g-factor=2.03 is assigned to symmetrical hexa-coordinate isolated Fe<sup>3+</sup> or the characteristic peak of polymeric iron ions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0018_R_d134249765e1268" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Commun.">18</a></sup></span> The narrower peak of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> indicates the more isolated Fe<sup>3+</sup> within Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0019_R_d134249765e1278" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Catal.">19</a></sup></span> This means that more [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies are formed in Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample, which is consistent with the ICP and TG analysis. And the peak with around g-factor=2.03 is relative to sample dehydration in previous reports,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0020_R_d134249765e1285" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Phys. Chem. Solids">20</a></sup></span> which confirms the water coordination with Fe in the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies. There exists small difference of g-factor between the two samples. The larger g-factor of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample verifies its lower valence state, compared with Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> samples. This may result from more net electrons, which participates in the neutralization potential of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample as the defect equation. Raman can reflect the valence state of PBAs to some extent.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0004_R_d134249765e1297" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Small">4</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0021_R_d134249765e1297" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a>-<a href="#anie202400214-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0023_R_d134249765e1297" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nano Energy">23</a></sup></span> The peaks of 2051.4 and 2052.1 cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to Fe(III)−CN of the two samples respectively, which are close to each other (Figure S11). The Fe(II) −CN peaks of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> locate on 2129.1 and 2093.6 cm<sup>−1</sup>. And the peaks of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>4</sub> locate on 2122.1 and 2092.3 cm<sup>−1</sup>. This difference may be due to the different content of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> in the two samples. The peak intensity of Fe<sup>3+</sup> was significantly higher than that of Fe<sup>2+</sup>, which should be ascribed to the oxidation of Fe elements accelerated by Raman laser.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">According to the results of MIBE-XPS, EPR, and Raman measurements, there are Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> in the both samples. However, the charge of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample are imbalance, even considering the valence state of whole Fe are +2 (Na<sub>0.64</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.52</sub> ⋅ □<sub>0.48</sub> ⋅ 2.08H<sub>2</sub>O). This should be ascribed to additional electrons fill the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies. Assuming that the valence state of whole Fe are +2, the defect equation can be written as Equation 3, based on Na<sub>2</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)]<sub>6</sub>. There are 0.56 net electrons containing in Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> unit cell. Because the valence state of Fe has both +2 and +3, there are actually more than 0.56 net electrons. The net electrons make Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample like n type semiconductor, which can improve the electronic conductance and benefit to rate performance. Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> (Na<sub>0.69</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.70</sub> ⋅ □<sub>0.30</sub> ⋅ 2.34H<sub>2</sub>O sample) may not include net electrons. Because the charge is balance, if considering that there are Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> simultaneously.
            <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202400214-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/94a0463c-68af-4a11-a6ff-47c9ce7bc7db/anie202400214-math-0003.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e53f7a9c-7636-4a1b-a4a1-79e2044daa5d/anie202400214-math-0004.png"></span><math></math>
: Na vacancy with a negative charge; <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4e07fa8e-695c-422d-b15d-8f036dda75b0/anie202400214-math-0005.png"></span><math></math>
: Na vacancy with four positive charges.</p>
         
         <p>In the micrometer scale, the morphology of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample are consisted of small particles and square column from the scanning electron microscope (SEM, Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0002">2f</a>). It still can be recognized the same morphology at the nanoscale (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0002">2g</a>). Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample displays nanoscale particles agglomeration (Figure S12). Transmission electron microscope (TEM) image (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0002">2h</a>) shows the square column of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample are hollow, which increases the contact area with electrolyte and shortens transmission path of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample seems still like nanoscale particles agglomeration (Figure S13). The different morphology of the two samples may relate to the high solubility of PBAs and different reaction in the Eg solvent. The element mapping of Fe, C, N, and O elements have significant correlation with the morphology of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> and Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0002">2i</a> and Figure S14), but Na element is an exception. Because the two samples are Na-poor state, it is consistent with the ICP results.</p>
         
         <p>It is obvious that Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample has excellent rate performance from cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0003">3a</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0003">3b</a>). The low redox pair peaks (marked as peak 1 and 1’) are corresponding to high-spin Fe connecting with N, and the higher peaks (marked as 2 and 2’) are corresponding to low-spin Fe connecting with C.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0024_R_d134249765e1576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Nat. Commun.">24</a></sup></span> The potential of peak 1 and 1’ appear a weak offset at 0.1–0.75 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. And there is almost no polarization of peaks 2 and 2’. At the scan rate of 1–10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the peaks of 1, 1’, 2, and 2’ appear obvious offset, but the polarization of peaks 2 and 2’ are lower than that of peaks 1 and 1’. The peaks polarization can indicate the electrochemical reaction rate. Therefore, the peaks 2 and 2’ show higher reaction rate than that of peaks of 1 and 1’. Furthermore, the peaks current of the four peaks is proportional to the power of the scan rate (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0003">3c</a>). The ratio of peaks 2 and 2’ are close to 1, which indicates capacitive-controlled process. And the peaks 1 and 1’ are within 0.5 and 1, which indicates the capacitive-controlled process and Faradaic intercalation process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0021_R_d134249765e1587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span> The slope of log(scan rate) and log(peak current) are decreasing with the increase of scan rate, which may be affected by diffusion process or Ohmic internal resistance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0025_R_d134249765e1590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Mater.">25</a></sup></span> Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample behaves high polarization at different scan rate (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0003">3d</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0003">3e</a>). Besides, all the peaks current of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample are lower than that of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample. This should be ascribed to the particle agglomeration and lower net electrons concentration of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202400214-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7e6c15d9-24b5-45d8-bc5d-5b450feba5db/anie202400214-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7e6c15d9-24b5-45d8-bc5d-5b450feba5db/anie202400214-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ad38ea71-a5cd-4743-92aa-36055ecd4cf7/anie202400214-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7e6c15d9-24b5-45d8-bc5d-5b450feba5db/anie202400214-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400214-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400214" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical kinetic characterization. (a), (b) CV measurements of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample at different scan rate; (c) Correspondence between log(peaks current) and log(scan rate); (d), (e) CV measurements of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample at different scan rate; (f) Arrhenius plots of ionic conductivity. (g), (h) GITT measurement of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> and Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample respectively.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Hebb-Wagner method and potentiostatic electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (PEIS) were used in the measurement of electronic and ionic conductivity of PBAs respectively, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0003">3f</a>, Table S9, and Figure S15. The ion conductivity and electronic conductivity are slightly higher than that of previous report, in which PBAs were prepared as solid-state electrolytes for sodium-ion batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0026_R_d134249765e1645" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">26</a></sup></span> Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was carried out to study the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0027_R_d134249765e1650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Determination of the Chemical Diffusion Coefficient of Lithium in LiFePO 4">27</a></sup></span> Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample has large Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion coefficient above 10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0003">3g</a>), which is beyond that of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0003">3h</a>). The different ion diffusion accounts for the large difference of CV measurement of the two samples. In addition, test time of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample is longer than that of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample at the same test current density, which means it has larger specific capacity. There is different behavior of the ion conductivity of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> and Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> samples in different measurement (GITT and PEIS), which may come from followed reasons. First, hollow structure of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> gives it a large specific surface to contact with electrolyte, which facilitates the ion transport GITT analysis. But the hollow structure will decrease effective ion transmission channel in PEIS analysis. Second, the PBAs samples may not satisfy the assumptions of GITT (the fixed active surface area and diffusion limited system). Besides, the GITT and PEIS obtain the ion conductivity from different mass transfer mode. Specifically, the GITT corresponds to diffusion, while the PEIS corresponds to migration.</p>
         
         <p>The in situ galvanostatic electrochemical impedance spectra (in situ-GEIS) was carried out to reveal the internal state relating to the different potential (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0004">4a</a> ~4n, Figure S16 and S17).<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0028_R_d134249765e1690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Nat. Commun.">28</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0029_R_d134249765e1690" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - The Carrier Transition from Li Atoms to Li Vacancies in Solid-State Lithium Alloy Anodes">29</a></sup></span> These two samples behave asymmetrically during charge and discharge process. The intersection points are 310 Ω, 290 Ω at 3.2 V, 3.6 V (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0004">4b</a>) respectively, between middle low frequency region and real axis of EIS during the charge process of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample. However, the intersection points are 450 Ω, 490 Ω at 3.2 V, 3.6 V during the discharge process, which is larger than that during the charge process. Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample is analogous to that. This phenomenon is quite similar to the results of CV measurements, in which the polarization of oxidation peaks is smaller than that of reduction peaks. The reason may come from the asymmetry of electron electronic and ionic conductivity during the charge and discharge process. The ionic conductivity is asymmetry during the charge and discharge process of the both samples from the results of GITT analysis (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0003">3g</a>, 3 h). And the asymmetry electronic conductivity should be attributed to that the two samples may be the n type semiconductor materials, which form Schottky Contact with current collector or conductive carbon. The current is asymmetry for Schottky Contact, between the forward bias (corresponding to the charge process of PBAs cathode) and reverse bias (corresponding to the discharge process of PBAs cathode). The current of forward bias is heavier than that of reverse bias, when the same bias voltage is applied. Therefore, the comprehensive asymmetry of ionic and electronic conductivity lead to the different impedance.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202400214-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/907d60bc-eea6-4b37-8ac5-1827a5c474e8/anie202400214-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/907d60bc-eea6-4b37-8ac5-1827a5c474e8/anie202400214-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/296d5daf-2251-4691-bcf1-f6e625ddc035/anie202400214-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/907d60bc-eea6-4b37-8ac5-1827a5c474e8/anie202400214-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400214-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400214" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>In situ-GEIS analysis. (a), (b) In situ-GEIS analysis of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample half battery; (c)~(g) DRT analysis based on the in situ-GEIS of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample half battery; (h), (i) In situ-GEIS analysis of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample half battery; (j)~(n) DRT analysis based on the in situ-GEIS of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample half battery.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Distribution of relaxation time (DRT) analysis is based on the in situ-GEIS measurement, which is using infinite parallel resistance (R) and capacitance (C) to fit complex system. Different relaxation time (τ) of RC correspond to different process of the research system.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0030_R_d134249765e1738" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Joule">30</a></sup></span> According to the previous reports,<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0030_R_d134249765e1741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Joule">30</a>-<a href="#anie202400214-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0032_R_d134249765e1741" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a></sup></span> the τ in Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0004">4c</a>~4g corresponds to electronic conductivity, particle and current collector contact, cathode electrolyte interface, charge transfer in electrodes, and solid-state diffusion in electrodes respectively. The DRT results of half batteries should be decoupled by excluding the effect of Na metal anode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0033_R_d134249765e1747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Power Sources">33</a></sup></span> Therefore, the EIS of Na metal symmetrical cells are measured after the same operating mode as half batteries. The influence of Na anode focuses on two process of cathode electrolyte interface and charge transfer in electrodes (Figure S18), whose resistance and τ are similar to that of the cathode materials. The electronic conductivity resistance of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample is smaller than that of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample during the whole charge and discharge process (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0004">4c</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0004">4j</a>). And the resistance of the two samples is asymmetric, which is smaller during charge process than that during discharge process. It confirms the Schottky Contact of PBAs and current collector or conductive carbon, which is consistent with the above analysis. The opposite results of DRT and PEIS about electronic conductivity (Figure S15, Table S9) may come from the different contact. The contact is Schottky Contact between stainless steels (SS) and Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> (or Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub>, PBAs). And the SS/PBAs/SS symmetry cells (Figure S19a) of PEIS make a double Schottky contact with opposite directions (Figure S19b). The excitation voltage of the contact is superposition of forward and reverse excitation voltages of single Schottky contact, which may exceed the stable voltage of PBAs. Besides, the hierarchical structure of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> may enable it to mix with conductive carbon fully, which is benefit to the higher electronic conductivity in DRT analysis. The resistance of particle and current collector contact of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample is smaller than that of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0004">4d</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0004">4k</a>), which should be attributed to the nanoscale particles agglomeration of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample. The cathode electrolyte interface of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample exhibits smaller resistance than that of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0004">4e</a>, 4 l), which is on account of the hierarchical hollow morphology of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample. There is larger τ of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample than that of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample, affected by the Na metal anode. Similarly, the resistance of charge transfer in Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample electrodes is smaller than that of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0004">4f</a>, 4 m). And there appear small resistance only at the end of the charge and discharge process or interval of two redox plateau. The resistance of solid-state diffusion in electrodes are large at the end of charge and discharge process (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0004">4g</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0004">4n</a>), which is consistent with phenomenon that ionic concentration affects the ionic transport rate. And the resistance of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample is smaller than that of Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample, which is coincident with the GITT results.</p>
         
         <p>The ex situ XRD characterization was conducted to reveal the crystal structure change (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0005">5a</a>, Figure S20) of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample. The crystal structure maintains the cubic phase in most potential. When the potential reaches 3.8 V, it appears tetragonal phase partly.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0008_R_d134249765e1820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">8</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0021_R_d134249765e1820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a></sup></span> It still maintains the mixture of cubic and tetragonal phase at the end of charge. During the charge process, the crystalline phase of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample shifts from the cubic phase to mixture of cubic and tetragonal phase, vice versa (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0005">5b</a>). The rate and long cycle performance of the samples are measured in half batteries. Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample half batteries deliver excellent rate performance and specific capacity, which is consistent with the above analysis. Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample behaves high specific capacity of 168 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at small current density of 0.025 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which realizes ultrahigh energy density over 500 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0005">5c</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0005">5d</a>). It still has 90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at huge current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Thanks to the n type semiconductor character, hierarchical hollow morphology, and high ionic diffusion. Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample appears the high specific capacity and excellent rate performance, which is more outstanding than most other published PBAs (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0005">5e</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0004_R_d134249765e1855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Small">4</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0008_R_d134249765e1855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">8</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0011_R_d134249765e1855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0021_R_d134249765e1855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0034_R_d134249765e1855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">34</a>-<a href="#anie202400214-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0038_R_d134249765e1855" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Lett.">38</a></sup></span> As for Eg-NaFeSO<sub>4</sub> sample, it only behaves 124 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.025 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and 70 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S21). Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample show high cycle stability. It maintains 70 % capacity retention rate at 2.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0005">5f</a>), which is comparable to the reported PBAs (Table S10) in recent years. The hollow morphology and [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies may take responsibility for the unsatisfactory cycle performance. Therefore, the comparative analysis (TEM and XRD) were conducted before and after different cycle. As shown in Figure S22, Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> samples appear new Na-rich rhombohedral phase and cubic phase change.<span><sup><a href="#anie202400214-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202400214-bib-0039_R_d134249765e1881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">39</a></sup></span> The lattice parameters of cubic phase increase after dozens of cycles, which causes characteristic peaks offset towards small angles. These changes result from the fact that more sodium ions enter the lattice, compared with the original Na-poor cubic phase. From the TEM analysis, the hollow structure is well maintained after different cycle (Figure S23).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202400214-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/281cd9c3-05de-4041-8945-130b635e817f/anie202400214-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/281cd9c3-05de-4041-8945-130b635e817f/anie202400214-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9fd31783-2e96-4ac5-a3d4-a41b99025746/anie202400214-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/281cd9c3-05de-4041-8945-130b635e817f/anie202400214-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400214-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400214" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Ex situ XRD and normal electrochemical measurement of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample. (a) Ex situ XRD measurement; (b) Schematic diagram of crystal structure change; (c), (d) Rate performance of half batteries; (e) Comparison between Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> and other reported PBAs; (f) Long cycle performance of half batteries.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The full batteries were assembled by Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample as cathode and hard carbon as anode (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0006">6a</a>). The energy density of the batteries reaches 353 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 25 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the mass of cathode). It still behaves about 170 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0006">6b</a>, <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0006">6c</a>). The full batteries maintain 71.4 % capacity retention rate at 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202400214-fig-0006">6d</a>). The poor cycle stability of hard carbon has a huge impact on the full batteries from the difference cycle stability of full and half batteries.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202400214-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4bb8fbed-a2a6-4106-8dba-7f1318bf7030/anie202400214-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4bb8fbed-a2a6-4106-8dba-7f1318bf7030/anie202400214-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e68cd4a0-d5ac-4c10-b37f-f12b9e892783/anie202400214-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4bb8fbed-a2a6-4106-8dba-7f1318bf7030/anie202400214-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202400214-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202400214" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performance of Eg-NaFeCl<sub>2</sub> sample and hard carbon full batteries. (a) Schematic diagram of the full batteries; (b), (c) Rate performance of the full batteries; (d) Long cycle performance of the full batteries.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202400214-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202400214-sec-0003-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we proposed that [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies are crucial point for the improvement of capacity and rate performance of PBAs cathode for the first time. As a proof of concept, a series of PBAs have been prepared by co-precipitation method. And then, a novel precipitation conversion method has been design, by which unique PBAs with a specific quantity of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>] vacancies was successfully synthesized. The DRT analysis confirms the Schottky Contact, which is consistent with the results of the defect equation based on the ICP, MIBE-XPS, and Raman results. Remarkably, assisting with the vacancies-regulation and hierarchical hollow morphology, the as-prepared PBAs have reached a unprecedent level of high capacity (168 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 25 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, close to its theoretical capacity 170 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), high rate performance (90 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and high energy density (over 500 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>). The unconventional vacancies strategy and synthesis method could inspire the different field of researches relating to PBAs or other materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202400214-sec-0004">
